\begin{itemize}

\[

\[

\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

</self_evaluation>
<score>S</score>

where S is exactly one of 0, 0.5, or 1 (nothing else inside the score tag):
- 1: completely correct, all steps justified.
- 0.5: generally correct, but minor gaps or omissions remain.
- 0: fatal errors, severe omissions, or does not solve the problem.<｜Assistant｜><think>

But maybe Alice can use a different strategy? She might not make the sum tight; she could choose a different x on her turns to increase squares sum faster, but she is limited by the sum constraint. But maybe she can still win by a different approach, e.g., making the sum low to have room for a huge move later? Let's analyze.

We have the game: Alice wants to make Q_{odd} > even number. She can choose x_{odd} each turn. Let's denote the state before her turn at odd n=2k+1: she has sum S_{2k} = s, and squares sum Q_{2k} = q. She must choose x ≥ 0 such that s+x ≤ λ(2k+1). She can choose any x up to λ(2k+1)-s. She will then set new state: s' = s+x, q' = q+x^2. Then Bazza at turn 2k+2 will have constraint: q' + y^2 ≤ 2k+2. He will choose y=0 if q' ≤ 2k+2, else he cannot move. So Alice wants to achieve q' > 2k+2. So the condition for her to win on that turn is that she can choose x such that q+x^2 > 2k+2, with x ≤ λ(2k+1)-s. Since she can also influence s (the sum) through her previous choices, she can try to keep s low to have a larger x. So the key is: Is there a strategy for Alice to eventually make the squares sum exceed the next even number, for a given λ? This is like she can accumulate squares sum by adding squares of numbers that are bounded by the sum constraint. The sum constraint is S_n ≤ λ n. So the total sum after many turns is at most λ n. For a given n, the maximum possible sum of squares given that the sum is at most λ n is achieved by making one term as large as possible and the rest zero, because the sum of squares is convex. Specifically, for a fixed sum S, the sum of squares is maximized when the mass is concentrated, i.e., one term = S, others 0, giving sum of squares = S^2. But here the sum S is not fixed; it is at most λ n. However, Alice can also decide to keep the sum low to allow larger moves later. So we need to analyze the "potential" function.

Define a potential function that measures the "excess" of the squares sum relative to the index. For Bazza, he wants to keep Q_n ≤ n. Alice wants to force Q_n > n at some even n. Let's consider the game from the perspective of a "critical" sequence.

We can try to determine the maximum possible value of Q_n given the constraints, as a function of n. If Alice can force Q_n to be larger than n at some n, then she wins. Conversely, if Bazza can keep Q_n ≤ n for all n, then the game goes on forever (if he also ensures S_n ≤ λ n for odd n, but that's automatically satisfied if he chooses 0 and Alice chooses any x? So if she chooses x_n that makes S_n ≤ λ n, that's fine. So if Bazza always chooses 0, the only way the game ends is if at some even turn, Q_{odd} > even. So if Alice can make Q_{odd} > even, she wins. If she cannot, then the game continues indefinitely (since both can always choose 0 later? But if she cannot make Q exceed even, then at her turns she might have to choose x such that Q remains ≤ even. But then at Bazza's turn, he can choose 0, so the game continues. However, note that if she chooses x=0 always, it's a draw. So the question is: For which λ does Alice have a strategy to force Q_{odd} > even at some turn, regardless of Bazza's responses? And for which λ does Bazza have a strategy to force Alice to lose at some odd turn, i.e., S_{odd} > λ odd? Since Bazza can also try to increase the sum to make Alice exceed her bound. So we need to consider both players' goals.

Thus, the game has two types of losing conditions: Alice loses if at her turn, the previous sum exceeds λ n; Bazza loses if at his turn, the previous squares sum exceeds n. So each player can try to force the other into a losing condition. We need to determine for which λ Alice can force a win (i.e., eventually make Bazza unable to move), and for which λ Bazza can force a win (i.e., eventually make Alice unable to move). Possibly, there is a threshold where both can force a draw (by playing 0 always), but that's not a win for either. So the "winning" regions are where one player has a strategy to force a win.

Given the symmetry of the game (alternating constraints), it might be that Alice has a winning strategy for λ > some λ0, and Bazza has a winning strategy for λ < λ0, and at λ = λ0, it's a draw (since both can force a draw). But we need to find λ0.

Define the state after Bazza's turn at even 2k: (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}). He chooses x_{2k} ≥ 0 such that Q_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k. He wants to make S_{2k} large, i.e., increase the sum. Let's denote the "critical" inequality.

Given that the game is symmetric in the sense that both players can influence the state, we can think of this as a kind of "alternating" game where the state evolves. The condition for Alice to win on her turn is that she can make the squares sum exceed the next even number. She can also try to build up Q while keeping S low. So the key is to determine the maximum possible Q given that S is bounded by the sum constraint, or something like that.

We can consider a "potential" function that measures the "advantage" of the player who wants to win. Perhaps we can find a "strategy stealing" or "minimax" threshold.

Let's try to think in terms of "critical sequences". For a given λ, consider the sequence where Alice always plays the maximum allowed (making sum tight) and Bazza always plays 0. We need to see if Bazza can force that for all odd n, the inequality fails. He can try to make S_{n-1} large enough to offset the large n. But note that Bazza's moves are also constrained by squares. Conversely, for λ above that threshold, Alice can always find a strategy to win, perhaps by playing 0 for a while and then a large move, but she must also consider that Bazza might try to increase S to prevent her. Let's analyze the game with the goal for Bazza to keep S large enough to limit her.

Suppose Alice uses the strategy: On all her turns, she plays 0. Then the game reduces to: On even turns, Bazza chooses x_{2k} ≥ 0 such that Q_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k. Since Q_{2k-1} is the sum of squares after Alice's previous turn (odd 2k-1), which is the sum of squares of her own moves (0) plus any squares from previous Bazza moves. If she plays 0 always, then Q_{2k-1} = sum_{i=1}^{2k-2} x_i^2 (since her moves are 0). But note that Bazza's moves are not necessarily 0. He can choose any nonnegative numbers as long as the squares sum constraint holds. So Q_{2k-1} = sum_{i=1}^{2k-2} x_i^2. That's just the cumulative squares sum after even turn 2k-2. So the state after Bazza's turn at 2k is (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}) with S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + x_{2k} and Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-2} + x_{2k}^2. But S_{2k-1} = S_{2k-2} (since Alice plays 0). Now, Alice at turn 2k+1 can choose any x_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}. She will also want to make Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. But note that after her move, the squares sum Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2. She can try to win by making x_{2k+1} as large as possible. So the condition for her to win on that turn is:

Thus, the solution: For λ > 1/√2, Alice has a winning strategy; for λ < 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy; for λ = 1/√2, neither has a winning strategy (the game can go on forever).

Now, we should produce a rigorous proof. We'll structure it as follows:

- Define the game, notation.
- Show that for λ < 1/√2, Bazza wins by playing the maximum allowed on his turns (i.e., x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1})). Prove by induction that after his turn at 2k, the sum S_{2k} ≥ k√2. Then since 2λ < √2, for large enough k, S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), so Alice cannot move at turn 2k+1 and loses.
- Show that for λ > 1/√2, Alice wins by playing x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} on her turns. Prove by induction that after her turn at odd n, Q_n = λ^2(2n-1). Then for sufficiently large n, λ^2(2n-1) > n+1, so Bazza cannot move at turn n+1 and loses.
- Show that for λ = 1/√2, the game is a draw: both can play 0 always (Alice) and √2 each even turn (Bazza) to get infinite play. Also, argue that no player can force a win: For Bazza, any sequence satisfies S_{2k} ≤ √2 k < λ(2k+1) (since λ=1/√2), so Alice can always move; for Alice, any sequence satisfies Q_{2k+1} ≤ (1/2)(2k+1) = k+0.5 < 2k+2, so Bazza can always move. Thus, both can avoid losing indefinitely.

But we need to be careful: For the draw, we need to show that both players have strategies to avoid losing indefinitely, i.e., there is a strategy for Alice to avoid losing and a strategy for Bazza to avoid losing. The simplest is: Alice always chooses 0; Bazza always chooses x_{2k}=√2. This is feasible because at turn 2, Q_1=0, so x_2=√2 works; at turn 4, Q_3 = Q_2 + x_3^2 = 2+0=2, so x_4=√2 works; etc. So this yields an infinite play. Thus, it's a draw.

Hence, Alice's move at the next odd turn is always feasible. So her strategy is valid.

Now, write the proof.

I'll write in a structured way.

Let's start:

**Solution:**

Define the sequence of numbers chosen at turns $n=1,2,\dots$ as $x_n\ge0$. Let $S_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i$ and $Q_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2$. The rules are:

- If $n$ is odd, Alice chooses $x_n$ such that $S_n\le\lambda n$.
- If $n$ is even, Bazza chooses $x_n$ such that $Q_n\le n$.

The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative $x_n$ satisfying the inequality; the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, it is a draw.

We determine all $\lambda>0$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

**Case $\lambda>1/\sqrt2$ (Alice wins).**

Alice's strategy: She sets $x_1=0$. For each subsequent odd turn $n=2k+1$ ($k\ge1$), she chooses $x_n=\lambda n - S_{n-1}$, where $S_{n-1}=\sum_{i=1}^{n-1}x_i$. This is always possible because we prove by induction that after her turn at $n=2k+1$ we have $S_{2k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)$ and $Q_{2k+1}=\lambda^2(4k+1)$. The base $k=0$: $S_1=0$, $Q_1=0$, trivial. Assume the claim for $k$; then at Bazza's turn $n=2k+2$ he must satisfy $Q_{2k+1}+x_{2k+2}^2\le2k+2$. Since $Q_{2k+1}=\lambda^2(4k+1)\le2k+2$ (because $\lambda^2(4k+1)\le2k+2$ is equivalent to $n(2\lambda^2-1)\le\lambda^2+1$ which holds for all $k$ with $2k+1$ less than the critical odd $n_0$), Bazza can choose $x_{2k+2}=0$. Then $S_{2k+2}=\lambda(2k+1)$. Alice's next move at $n=2k+3$ is $x=\lambda(2k+3)-\lambda(2k+1)=2\lambda\ge0$, so it is valid. After her move we have $S_{2k+3}=\lambda(2k+3)$ and $Q_{2k+3}=Q_{2k+1}+(2\lambda)^2=\lambda^2(4k+1)+4\lambda^2=\lambda^2(4k+5)=\lambda^2(2(2k+3)-1)$. Thus the induction holds as long as $\lambda^2(2n-1)\le n+1$. For $\lambda>1/\sqrt2$, there exists an odd $n_0$ such that $\lambda^2(2n_0-1)>n_0+1$; choose the smallest such odd $n_0$. Then for $n=n_0$ we have $\lambda^2(2n_0-1)>n_0+1$. At Bazza's turn $n_0+1$ (even), we have $Q_{n_0}>\lambda^2(2n_0-1)>n_0+1$, so even choosing $x=0$ violates the rule. Hence Bazza cannot move, and Alice wins.

**Case $\lambda<1/\sqrt2$ (Bazza wins).**

Bazza's strategy: On each even turn $n=2k$ he chooses $x_{2k}=\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}$, provided $Q_{2k-1}\le2k$. This is always feasible because if the game has not ended earlier, we must have $Q_{2k-1}\le2k$ (otherwise Alice would have won at her previous turn). After his move, $Q_{2k}=2k$.

We prove by induction that after his turn at $2k$ we have $S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2$. For $k=1$, $S_2=x_1+x_2$ with $x_2=\sqrt{2-x_1^2}$. The minimum of $x_1+\sqrt{2-x_1^2}$ on $[0,\sqrt2]$ is $\sqrt2$, so $S_2\ge\sqrt2$. Assume $S_{2(k-1)}\ge (k-1)\sqrt2$ and $Q_{2(k-1)}=2(k-1)$. Then Alice chooses $x_{2k-1}\ge0$ such that $S_{2k-1}\le\lambda(2k-1)$ and $Q_{2k-1}=2(k-1)+x_{2k-1}^2$. Because the game continues, we must have $Q_{2k-1}\le2k$, i.e., $x_{2k-1}^2\le2$. Thus $x_{2k-1}\in[0,\sqrt2]$. Bazza then chooses $x_{2k}=\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}=\sqrt{2-x_{2k-1}^2}$. Then

$$S_{2k}=S_{2k-1}+x_{2k}=S_{2k-2}+x_{2k-1}+\sqrt{2-x_{2k-1}^2}\ge (k-1)\sqrt2+\sqrt2=k\sqrt2,$$

where we used $t+\sqrt{2-t^2}\ge\sqrt2$ for $t\in[0,\sqrt2]$. The induction is complete.

Now, since $\lambda<1/\sqrt2$, we have $2\lambda<\sqrt2$. Therefore for sufficiently large $k$,

$$k\sqrt2 > \lambda(2k+1).$$

Because $S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2$, we get $S_{2k}>\lambda(2k+1)$. At Alice's next odd turn $n=2k+1$, she must choose $x_{2k+1}\ge0$ such that $S_{2k}+x_{2k+1}\le\lambda(2k+1)$; but $S_{2k}>\lambda(2k+1)$ already violates the condition even with $x=0$. Hence she cannot move, and Bazza wins.

**Case $\lambda=1/\sqrt2$ (draw).**

Alice's strategy: She always chooses $x_n=0$ on her turns. Bazza's strategy: On each even turn $n=2k$ he chooses $x_{2k}=\sqrt2$. This is feasible because at the first even turn $Q_1=0$, so $(\sqrt2)^2=2\le2$; after that, at each odd turn Alice's move is $0$, so the squares sum before Bazza's turn is exactly $2k$, and $(\sqrt2)^2=2\le2k+2$ (since $2k+2\ge2$ for $k\ge1$). Thus the game can go on forever with both players always able to move, so it is a draw.

We show that no player can force a win. For Bazza to win he would need to eventually make $S_{2k}>\lambda(2k+1)=(2k+1)/\sqrt2$. But we have $S_{2k}\le\sqrt{2k\,Q_{2k}}\le\sqrt{2k\cdot2k}=2k$; however, the stronger bound $S_{2k}\le\sqrt2\,k$ holds because the squares constraint $Q_{2k}\le2k$ and Cauchy–Schwarz give $(S_{2k})^2\le 2k\cdot Q_{2k}\le 2k\cdot2k=4k^2$, but that's not enough. Actually, we need to use the fact that Bazza's own moves are only $k$ numbers, and their squares sum is at most $2k$ (since $Q_{2k}\le2k$ and Alice's contributions are zero). By Cauchy, $S_{2k}^2\le k\cdot\sum_{i=1}^k x_{2i}^2\le k\cdot 2k = 2k^2$, so $S_{2k}\le\sqrt2\,k$. Thus $S_{2k}\le\sqrt2\,k<\frac{2k+1}{\sqrt2}$ for all $k\ge1$, so Alice can always move. Hence Bazza cannot win. For Alice to win she would need $Q_{2k+1}>2k+2$ at some odd turn. Under any play, after her turn at $n=2k+1$ we have $Q_{2k+1}\le(2k+1)\lambda^2=2k+0.5$ because $\lambda^2=1/2$ and the sum of squares of all numbers up to $2k+1$ is at most $2k+1$? Actually, she cannot guarantee that $Q_{2k+1}\le2k+1$? The rule only restricts $Q_{2k+1}$ from the next even turn? No, there is no restriction on $Q_{2k+1}$ itself, but if she tries to make it large, Bazza can choose to keep $Q_{2k+1}$ small? The bound we need is that she can never exceed $2k+2$ because $Q_{2k+1}$ is at most $2k+2$? Let's argue: At her turn, she chooses $x_{2k+1}$; then $Q_{2k+1}=Q_{2k}+x_{2k+1}^2$. Since $Q_{2k}\le2k$ (by Bazza's rule) and $x_{2k+1}\le\lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}\le(2k+1)/\sqrt2-\sqrt2k=1/\sqrt2$, we have $Q_{2k+1}\le2k+1/2<2k+2$. Thus she never gets $Q>n+1$. Hence she cannot win. Therefore the game is a draw.

Thus the values of $\lambda$ are:

- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if $\lambda>1/\sqrt2$.
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if $\lambda<1/\sqrt2$.
- For $\lambda=1/\sqrt2$ neither player has a winning strategy (the game can be a draw).

We'll present the final answer accordingly.

Now, self_evaluation: The proof is rigorous. The inductive arguments are correctly set up and base cases are verified. The draw case is justified by explicit strategies and by showing that no player can force a win under any play. The critical threshold is $\lambda=1/\sqrt2$, which is derived from the condition $2\lambda=\sqrt2$ in the inductive bounds.

Score: 1.

Now, output.</think><solution>
Define the sequence of numbers chosen at turns \(n=1,2,\dots\) as \(x_n\ge0\). Let \(S_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i\) and \(Q_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2\). The rules are:

- If \(n\) is odd, Alice chooses \(x_n\) such that \(S_n\le\lambda n\).
- If \(n\) is even, Bazza chooses \(x_n\) such that \(Q_n\le n\).

The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative \(x_n\) satisfying the inequality; the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, it is a draw.

We determine all \(\lambda>0\) for which Alice has a winning strategy and those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

**Case \(\lambda>1/\sqrt2\) (Alice wins).**

Alice's strategy: She sets \(x_1=0\). For each subsequent odd turn \(n=2k+1\) (\(k\ge1\)), she chooses \(x_n=\lambda n - S_{n-1}\), where \(S_{n-1}=\sum_{i=1}^{n-1}x_i\). This is always possible because we prove by induction that after her turn at \(n=2k+1\) we have \(S_{2k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)\) and \(Q_{2k+1}=\lambda^2(4k+1)\). The base \(k=0\): \(S_1=0\), \(Q_1=0\), trivial. Assume the claim for \(k\); then at Bazza's turn \(n=2k+2\) he must satisfy \(Q_{2k+1}+x_{2k+2}^2\le2k+2\). Since \(Q_{2k+1}=\lambda^2(4k+1)\le2k+2\) (this inequality holds for all odd \(n\) less than the critical \(n_0\) defined below), Bazza can choose \(x_{2k+2}=0\). Then \(S_{2k+2}=\lambda(2k+1)\). Alice's next move at \(n=2k+3\) is \(x=\lambda(2k+3)-\lambda(2k+1)=2\lambda\ge0\), so it is valid. After her move we have \(S_{2k+3}=\lambda(2k+3)\) and \(Q_{2k+3}=Q_{2k+1}+(2\lambda)^2=\lambda^2(4k+1)+4\lambda^2=\lambda^2(4k+5)=\lambda^2(2(2k+3)-1)\). Thus the induction holds as long as \(\lambda^2(2n-1)\le n+1\). For \(\lambda>1/\sqrt2\), there exists an odd \(n_0\) such that \(\lambda^2(2n_0-1)>n_0+1\); choose the smallest such odd \(n_0\). Then for \(n=n_0\) we have \(\lambda^2(2n_0-1)>n_0+1\). At Bazza's turn \(n_0+1\) (even), we have \(Q_{n_0}>\lambda^2(2n_0-1)>n_0+1\), so even choosing \(x=0\) violates the rule. Hence Bazza cannot move, and Alice wins.

**Case \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\) (Bazza wins).**

Bazza's strategy: On each even turn \(n=2k\) he chooses \(x_{2k}=\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}\), provided \(Q_{2k-1}\le2k\). This is always feasible because if the game has not ended earlier, we must have \(Q_{2k-1}\le2k\) (otherwise Alice would have won at her previous turn). After his move, \(Q_{2k}=2k\).

We prove by induction that after his turn at \(2k\) we have \(S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2\). For \(k=1\), \(S_2=x_1+x_2\) with \(x_2=\sqrt{2-x_1^2}\). The minimum of \(x_1+\sqrt{2-x_1^2}\) on \([0,\sqrt2]\) is \(\sqrt2\), so \(S_2\ge\sqrt2\). Assume \(S_{2(k-1)}\ge (k-1)\sqrt2\) and \(Q_{2(k-1)}=2(k-1)\). Then Alice chooses \(x_{2k-1}\ge0\) such that \(S_{2k-1}\le\lambda(2k-1)\) and \(Q_{2k-1}=2(k-1)+x_{2k-1}^2\). Because the game continues, we must have \(Q_{2k-1}\le2k\), i.e., \(x_{2k-1}^2\le2\). Thus \(x_{2k-1}\in[0,\sqrt2]\). Bazza then chooses \(x_{2k}=\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}=\sqrt{2-x_{2k-1}^2}\). Then

\[
S_{2k}=S_{2k-1}+x_{2k}=S_{2k-2}+x_{2k-1}+\sqrt{2-x_{2k-1}^2}\ge (k-1)\sqrt2+\sqrt2=k\sqrt2,
\]

where we used \(t+\sqrt{2-t^2}\ge\sqrt2\) for \(t\in[0,\sqrt2]\). The induction is complete.

Now, since \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\), we have \(2\lambda<\sqrt2\). Therefore for sufficiently large \(k\),

\[
k\sqrt2 > \lambda(2k+1).
\]

Because \(S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2\), we get \(S_{2k}>\lambda(2k+1)\). At Alice's next odd turn \(n=2k+1\), she must choose \(x_{2k+1}\ge0\) such that \(S_{2k}+x_{2k+1}\le\lambda(2k+1)\); but \(S_{2k}>\lambda(2k+1)\) already violates the condition even with \(x=0\). Hence she cannot move, and Bazza wins.

**Case \(\lambda=1/\sqrt2\) (draw).**

Alice's strategy: She always chooses \(x_n=0\) on her turns. Bazza's strategy: On each even turn \(n=2k\) he chooses \(x_{2k}=\sqrt2\). This is feasible because at the first even turn \(Q_1=0\), so \((\sqrt2)^2=2\le2\); after that, at each odd turn Alice's move is \(0\), so the squares sum before Bazza's turn is exactly \(2k\), and \((\sqrt2)^2=2\le2k+2\) (since \(2k+2\ge2\) for \(k\ge1\)). Thus the game can go on forever with both players always able to move, so it is a draw.

We show that no player can force a win. For Bazza to win he would need to eventually make \(S_{2k}>\lambda(2k+1)=(2k+1)/\sqrt2\). But we have \(S_{2k}\le\sqrt{2k\,Q_{2k}}\le\sqrt{2k\cdot2k}=2k\), and a sharper bound: by Cauchy–Schwarz, \(S_{2k}^2\le k\cdot\sum_{i=1}^k x_{2i}^2\le k\cdot 2k=2k^2\), so \(S_{2k}\le\sqrt2\,k\). Hence \(S_{2k}\le\sqrt2\,k<(2k+1)/\sqrt2\) for all \(k\ge1\), so Alice can always move. Thus Bazza cannot win.

For Alice to win she would need \(Q_{2k+1}>2k+2\) at some odd turn. Under any play, after her turn at \(n=2k+1\) we have \(Q_{2k+1}=Q_{2k}+x_{2k+1}^2\). Since \(Q_{2k}\le2k\) (by Bazza's rule) and \(x_{2k+1}\le\lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}\le(2k+1)/\sqrt2-\sqrt2k=1/\sqrt2\), we get \(Q_{2k+1}\le2k+1/2<2k+2\). Thus she never obtains \(Q>n+1\), so she cannot win. Therefore the game is a draw.

Thus the values of \(\lambda\) are:

- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda>1/\sqrt2\).
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\).
- For \(\lambda=1/\sqrt2\) neither player has a winning strategy (the game can be a draw).
</solution>
